# 悟真篇注疏

The Imperial *Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*
*Masters Section, Volume 14*
*Annotated Commentary on the "Awakening to Reality"*
*Daoist School*
*Abstract*

We, your ministers, respectfully note: *The Annotated Commentary on the "Awakening to Reality"* comprises three scrolls, with an appended *Detailed Explication of Direct Pointers* in one scroll. The work was composed by Zhang Boduan of the Song dynasty, annotated by Weng Baoguang, and later supplemented with additional exegesis by Dai Qizong.

Boduan, also named Yongcheng and styled Pingshu, was a native of Tiantai. During the Xining reign, he traveled to Shu, where he encountered an extraordinary person who transmitted to him the secrets of the Golden Elixir’s firing times. In the Yuanfeng reign, he attained the Dao in Jinghu, leaving behind a gatha on non-arising before passing away. His disciples cremated him, and what remained resembled the śarīra of the Buddhist tradition—countless in number. Seven years later, he was seen again in Wangwu Mountain, hence the popular legend of his ascension as an immortal.

This text elucidates the essentials of the Golden Elixir, standing alongside the *Triple Concordance Canon* as a foundational work revered by Daoists. Where it states, *"To know the source of the medicinal stream, seek only in the southwest—your native land,"* it echoes the *Triple Concordance Canon*’s *"Three days emerge as the fresh dawn; the Metal of the West is the barrier."* Where it speaks of *"a medicinal weight of one catty requiring two eights,"* it aligns with the *Triple Concordance Canon*’s *"Above, the trigram ☱ (Dui) counts eight; below, the trigram ☶ (Gen) also counts eight."* Where it declares, *"The three, five, and one—these three characters—are truly rare to comprehend in ancient or modern times,"* it mirrors the *Triple Concordance Canon*’s *"The three and five unite as one; the essence of Heaven and Earth—this can be transmitted orally but not in writing."* Where it advises, *"Wood born of fire inherently conceals its edge; one must subdue it to seek the Golden Duke,"* it reflects the *Triple Concordance Canon*’s *"The maiden of the River (mercury) takes flight when touched by fire; to restrain her, the Yellow Sprout serves as the root."*

These mutual elucidations delve profoundly into the mysteries, yet few cultivators of the Dao attain true understanding. During the Qiandao reign, Weng Baoguang of Xiangchuan divided the text into three sections, composing annotations to expound its meaning. He further appended *A Detailed Explication of Direct Pointers from "Awakening to Reality."* After prolonged circulation, the work was mistakenly attributed to Xue Daoguang, obscuring Baoguang’s name. By the Zhishun reign of the Yuan dynasty, Dai Qizong of Jiqing obtained the authentic version and meticulously revised it, establishing it as Baoguang’s annotation. He then composed a commentary to accompany it. Both men possessed deep mastery of elixir arts, grounding their explanations in solid principles.

As they state: *"Borrowing the true yin and true yang as two substances, one seizes the single qi of Heaven and Earth to forge the elixir, returning it to the Cinnabar Field and Sea of Qi within. There, it governs the postnatal qi of the entire body, drawing it inward as stars gather around the North Star."* Their discourse indeed encapsulates the secrets of elixir arts. Among the many commentaries on *Awakening to Reality*, none surpass this edition in clarity and precision. Thus, we include and preserve it to represent one of Daoism’s essential teachings.

Respectfully submitted for imperial review in the seventh month of the forty-fifth year of the Qianlong reign.
*Chief Compilers:*
*Minister* Ji Yun
*Minister* Lu Xixiong
*Minister* Sun Shiyi

**Chief Collator:**
*Minister* Lu Feichi

**Original Preface to the *Annotations and Commentaries on Awakening to Reality***

*Awakening to Reality* divides the Dao into two schools—*xing* (nature) and *ming* (life)—instructing practitioners to advance each within its proper domain. It further distinguishes inner and outer elixirs, guiding them to progress in unison. Thus, it stands as the foundational scripture of elixir arts for the ages, a teaching to be transmitted alongside the *Zhouyi Cantong Qi* for eternity. Before Ye Wenshu’s annotations, the text circulated among masters without explanatory notes, leaving its meanings obscure. After Ye’s commentary, later generations, though obscured by the Dao, failed to discern its errors.

Of the dozens of commentaries I have examined, all offer one-sided interpretations through solitary cultivation or misguidedly annotate it with peripheral arts, causing the great Way of the Golden Elixir to fade from the world. Seekers grope in darkness, finding no path to its gate, while oral transmissions from masters have nearly vanished. With no masters to follow, how can one hear the teachings? Thus, those who hear them are few—how then could there be those who practice them?

The commentary attributed to Shi Zixian merely treasures the words of the Perfected One, unaware that later generations plagiarized Weng Baoguang’s annotations, falsely attributing them to Daoguang. I have meticulously distinguished these at the end of the text.

The *Wumingzi* was also a disciple of the Perfected One. In the *gui-si* year of the Qiandao reign (1173), upon seeing the errors in Wenshu’s annotations, he feared the great Way would be further obscured. Thus, he composed *Explanations of the Meanings*, elucidating the profound principles and expounding the three vehicles’ truths, fully revealing the celestial mechanisms to clarify the master’s safeguards.

During my time in Ruiyang, I was shown several passages from Shi Zixian’s commentary on *Awakening to Reality*. Its annotations differed from other schools, and when compared with the teachings of my father and master, none aligned. I harbored deep doubts. Upon reading it repeatedly, I began to glimpse its subtleties. Later, obtaining the full text, I pored over it day and night, scrutinizing each word and sentence, engaging in prolonged reflection with my elder brother, Zhongfu, the County Magistrate. Through years of questioning, answering, debating, and verifying, I realized it harmonized with the *Cantong Qi* and the great elixir’s safeguards.

Though I had long studied the *Cantong Qi* and the mysteries of the Daoist teachings, my karmic conditions had not yet aligned, and I had no other practice. It was only through *Awakening to Reality* that I came to understand the safeguards. Yet even then, its distinctions between inner and outer elixirs, and the mysteries of the momentary alchemy, remained unclear. How much more so for others?

The immortals guarded their secrets, and no texts described them. With no master to consult and no texts to study, my heart remained fixed on this pursuit, never wavering. Heaven guided my resolve: in the summer of the *xin-wei* year of the Zhishun reign (1331), I met a master and received the oral transmission. Returning home, I shared it with my brother, who rejoiced without sleep. Later, upon seeing the *Wumingzi*’s annotations, it was as if they matched perfectly. I then knew that celestial immortality could be learned, that the primordial spirit was deathless, and that the immortals Zhong and Lü shared this same Way. With but a moment’s effort, one could ascend to the sacred realm—no wonder the heavens had sworn never to transmit it to mortals!

Alas! Since the *gui-hai* year of the Yanyou reign (1313), when I served as Professor of the Shaoxing Prefecture School, I have been fifty-two years old. Through relentless hardships, I nearly lost my life, and so I resolved to abandon fame and profit, dedicating myself wholly to the completion of *xing* and *ming*. My devotion was deep, my study diligent—I did not betray the Daoist heart. Though my master’s teachings led me through favorable circumstances, I remained untroubled by worldly burdens, my official rank rising no more than a few grades, my wealth never exceeding a hundred gold. What benefit did this fleeting life bring me? Now, knowing the truth yet failing to refine myself would be the height of folly—a single misstep in this human form, and a thousand kalpas would pass before another chance. How much rarer still to hear the Dao!

After hearing the Dao, I vowed to universally deliver all beings. Yet in the stillness of midnight, I dared not recklessly divulge celestial secrets—how then could I guide others? To tread the path without fear of heavenly retribution would violate my master’s oath. Thus, I have set it forth in writing and spoken it aloud. The world has long been deluded—who would believe? Therefore, I have compiled the refutations of heterodox schools by past patriarchs, so that practitioners may recognize their futility in attaining celestial immortality. This may prompt reflection on the true path to the Dao. Hence, I title it *Dispelling Delusions in the Study of Immortality*.

Once delusions are dispelled, how then to guide others into the mysteries of the Dao? I selected ten scriptures and Daoist classics of shared principles, using the master’s secret transmissions to elucidate the immortals’ intent, composing annotations to clarify the true path of immortal arts. Thus, I title it *The Orthodox Lineage of Immortal Study*, which is recorded in a separate collection.

Unexpectedly, my elder brother passed away before we could refine our practice together, deepening my vigilance. As my years advance, I redouble my resolve to hasten my cultivation. Finding the *Wumingzi*’s annotations occasionally unclear or its symbolic imagery incomplete, I composed commentaries for each chapter to illuminate its unexhausted subtleties, revealing the symbolic principles of the Golden Elixir and their interdependent functions.

Moreover, *Awakening to Reality* describes distinct methods for inner and outer elixirs, often using similar phrases with divergent meanings. It speaks of "establishing the furnace and tripod" yet also of "idly guarding the elixir furnace without firewood or charcoal." It claims the elixir "forms in an instant" yet also "ripens in ten months." Sometimes a single object illustrates a teaching, yet the applied methods differ; other times, multiple names serve as metaphors, yet the object remains the same. Such variations abound, easily misleading readers. Some verify one passage only to err in another, or grasp one meaning while losing another. This leads seekers to mistake partial truths for the whole, falling into heterodox paths. The more they know, the more entrenched their delusions become. They err in their own practice and mislead others, deepening the confusion—how lamentable!

Now, I have categorized the poems and lyrics, grouping them under distinct headings. Reading the main text alone reveals the general meaning, but the annotations and commentaries make it even clearer, rendering the path plainly visible. Those who obtain this book are destined for immortality; those who hear this teaching share its fate. Those who hear it and cultivate without doubt will cast aside worldly attachments, embrace unity, and empty the heart, fully realizing the truths of the three vehicles. They will not squander their lives but will transmit the immortals’ teachings, striving diligently without negligence.

As for the supreme vehicle, it must be self-realized and cannot be transmitted through words or language. Those who mistake oral teachings for the Dao fall into the emptiness of external paths, confusing it with the mysteries of immortal arts. Both the two schools and the two elixirs, when practiced in isolation, lead many into the currents of delusion. It is my hope that students of immortality will devote themselves to harmonious progress, fusing their cultivation. Only then can their celestial ranks differ, and I may repay, in some small measure, the kindness of my master, my true father, and my brother.

Respectfully inscribed in the first year of the Zhiyuan reign (1264) by Kongxuanzi Dai Qizong, courtesy name Tongfu.

On the day of Xu Zhenjun’s ascension to immortality, he declared: *"One thousand two hundred and forty years after my ascension, within the Five Mounds, eight hundred will attain immortality. In the middle of the Yu River, a great isle will emerge, spanning the river’s mouth—this will be the time."*

In the year of *jiazi* during the Chunxi reign (1174), one thousand two hundred and twenty-six years have now passed since Xu Zhenjun’s ascension. The great isle spanning the Yu River appears as if by divine decree—ah! Heaven raised Jingyang to preside over immortal affairs during the Jin dynasty, and now this portent unfolds as foretold. I know that within the Five Mounds, those who advance in this Dao are either awakened disciples or their descendants, for Heaven shall spread the true wind across the cosmos and establish the immortal realm in Yuzhang. How majestic is our Song dynasty, which produced the awakened one to govern the celestial registers and secretly assist the imperial house! Otherwise, how could he have received the title of *Zixuan* from the nine heavens?

The immortal Wuzhen, having heard the Dao atop Mount Qingcheng and refined the elixir between Jing and Hu, transmitted his teachings once to give rise to Guangyi Zi, and again to give rise to Wuming Zi. Truly, this is a land of immortals, where spiritual essence gathers and genius is nurtured within the Five Mounds, foretelling the immortal realm of Hongdu. Yet alas, many have misinterpreted Wuzhen’s single work—how can one reverse the raging tide or dam the hundred rivers to flow eastward?

Wuming Zi took up his brush to annotate it, each word like unrefined gold, each sentence like uncut jade, rendering great merit to this Dao. One day, he showed it to me, and I composed a preface outlining its general principles. The Purple Yang Elder of Wuling, Chen Daling, wrote the preface.

Kongxuan Zi says: *"Refining the elixir between Jing and Hu"*—according to Bai Yuchan’s *Xishan Fayu*, Zhang Pingshu succeeded only after encountering Ma Duyun, and Daoguang achieved the elixir only after meeting Zhang Huanwei. Thus, refining the elixir in Jinghu follows the method of Ma Chuhou. After the Perfected One attained the Dao, he again met Ma Chuhou in Hedong. Before departing, he entrusted the *Wuzhen Pian* to Chuhou, who later transmitted it in detail, as recorded in Lu Yanfu’s account.

The first transmission gave rise to Guangyi Zi. Liu Yongnian, who styled himself Shunli Guangyi Zi, published the *Cantong Qi* with chapter divisions and *Tongzhen Yi* in the *renshen* year of Shaoxing (1152). In his preface, he wrote: *"The Cantong Qi* illustrates the mechanism of the golden elixir in full. Those who fail to thoroughly explore this scripture merely follow its superficial allure, refining elixirs from metals and minerals with ordinary fire—how erroneous!"* Earlier, in the *wuwu* year of Shaoxing (1138), Yongnian had encountered a perfected one who personally transmitted oral instructions, admonishing him: *"Simply guard the Cantong Qi, for it aligns with my teachings."* Yongnian diligently followed his master’s words, and upon studying it, the principles became clear, leaving no doubt. He then gathered countless versions, meticulously collated and printed them, making them available to those of sincere interest so they might grasp the true subtleties—was this not a great merit?

When I, Qizong, first saw the printed edition, the imperial library version had not yet been released, and its text still contained errors. In reading the *Cantong Qi*, one should regard Bao Zhongqi’s collated secret library edition as authoritative, though even his version contains minor discrepancies. To clarify the *Tongzhen Yi*, one must rely on Peng Zhenyi’s annotated main text. I have therefore added annotations and comparisons to the original text.

The immortal Chen Xianweng of Ziyang, a native of Wuyi, left behind recorded sayings divided into sixty-four discourses, distinguishing the two schools of *xing* and *ming* and the two elixirs of internal and external. He also said: *"Without reading the ancient text *Longhu Shangjing*, one cannot know the subtleties of the two vapors of metal and water. Without reading the *Cantong Qi*, one cannot know the essence of the sun and moon’s one *jin*. Without reading the *Wuzhen Pian*, one cannot know the divine power of the two chapters of gold and jade. This Dao is the subtlest of the subtle, the most mysterious of the mysterious."*

One must gather the undivided *qi* of the dragon and tiger, absorb the nascent essence of the *kun* snake. Among the three hundred lines, whether civil or martial, within the sixty-four hexagrams, *Zhun* and *Meng* form the elixir in an instant and complete the Dao in nine years. It is also said: *"The Dao comes from within—where to place the furnace and tripod is external. The Dao comes from without—where true lead and true mercury reside is internal. One *jin* within, one *jin* without: purple gold transforms into purple powder, and purple powder transforms into the golden elixir."* This is the distinction between the internal and external elixirs of the prior heavens. Though these two emerge from the same source, they differ in name and function. The Dao of internal and external is thus perfected, complete, and beyond augmentation.

When the external elixir returns to the internal elixir, the internal connects with the external, and both must be in harmony. From this, we see that the Dao of Ziyang aligns with that of Wuzhen.

The Master once told me: *"The immortal of Tiantai attained the Dao and received his mandate from the Supreme Emperor as the Purple Void Perfected One, secretly aiding the imperial house. He often appeared and vanished in the world, yet none recognized him. In his youth, the immortal studied alongside Cheng Zu at the Imperial Academy. While Cheng did not pass the examinations, the immortal was born with a spiritual root. Upon reading Buddhist texts, he suddenly felt the sorrow of the bamboo’s bondage, instantly awakening to the unborn and directly transcending into the true emptiness of the pure nature-sea. In his later years, he met the Elder of Qingcheng in Chengdu and fully grasped the wondrous subtleties of the golden elixir, thoroughly understanding the mechanism of yin-yang inversion and the principle of heaven-earth’s cyclic generation. Thus, he cultivated the true and returned to life, refining his form to ascend into the formless, embracing the One with an empty heart, and perfecting both *xing* and *ming* in quiescence. Thereby, his form and spirit became wondrous, merging with the Dao in truth, transforming endlessly and universally manifesting in the dharma-realm to broadly deliver the deluded. During the Yuanfeng era, he and Liu Fengzhen’s disciples widely propagated Buddhist teachings, leaving a gatha on the unborn before entering quiescence. Fengzhen’s disciples cremated his remains and obtained hundreds of relics, some as large as fox nuts. Seven years later, Fengzhen’s disciples arrived at Wangwu Mountain and again encountered the immortal as before—this further demonstrated his wondrous unity of form and spirit, the perfection of both *xing* and *ming*."*

The immortal was endowed with a benevolent and magnanimous nature, thoroughly investigating principle and exhausting *xing* to attain *ming*. The three schools converged in him, their wondrous functions undifferentiated. Yet he did not wish to cultivate only himself, so he composed the *Wuzhen Pian* to instruct later learners. He began by enticing them with the Dao of immortality and the path of cultivation, placing the art of the golden elixir at the forefront of the work. He concluded by dispelling illusions with the true suchness of emptiness, ending the volume with Chan songs. The essentials of the golden elixir lie in the two substances of true yin and true yang in the proportions of two and eight, establishing the furnace and tripod to lure the prior-heaven’s *qi* into them, transforming it into a grain as small as a millet seed, called the *Taiyi Han Zhen*. Thus, he first arranged sixteen seven-character quatrains to represent the numbers of true yin and true yang. Next, he composed a five-character quatrain to represent the singularity of *Taiyi*—the single grain of the golden elixir. After obtaining this grain and ingesting it into the cinnabar field, one then circulates the fire in accordance with the sixty-four hexagrams. Therefore, he appended sixty-four quatrains to correspond to the sixty-four hexagrams of the *Zhouyi*.

The workings of the celestial fire span ten months, with bathing periods included, totaling twelve months. Thus, twelve *Xijiangyue* lyrics were appended to correspond to the twelve months of the celestial cycle. When the ten months of practice are complete and the embryo is fully formed, the body transforms into pure yang *qi*. Therefore, eighty-one regulated poems were composed in total, symbolizing the pure yang number of nine nines.

Once the body has transformed into *qi*, one then embraces the primordial for nine years, refining *qi* into spirit, and uniting spirit with the Dao. Thus, both form and spirit attain perfection, ascending into the formless and merging with the true Dao, imperceptible and unfathomable. Hence, both form and spirit, nature and life, all return to the ultimate source of emptiness and quiescence. Therefore, thirty-six Chan songs and verses on nature and the Dao conclude the volume.

The above all follows the symbolic progression of the great elixir’s stages. As for the ingredients, fire phases, and oral instructions—every subtle detail is embedded within the songs and verses. Readers may naturally uncover their meaning through the text.

Later transmissions, however, have grown increasingly widespread, with textual structures and sequences differing greatly and errors multiplying. Only the family copy preserved by Lu Sicheng, grandson of the *Longtu* scholar Lu, remains authentic—this being the very text personally transmitted by the immortal elder. Sicheng himself appended a detailed preface to the volume’s end.

During my travels to Dongting, I obtained this authentic copy and corrected its errors. Only then did I grasp the immortal elder’s intent: the sequence and chapters all correspond to the symbolic methods of the golden elixir. Though the text is concise, its profound principles are fully expounded; though its allusions are subtle, its analogies are intimate and direct. Truly, it is the authentic guide for scholars and the essential compendium of all scriptures.

Yet many of today’s scholars pursue heterodox paths, misapplying their interpretations. Some conflate it with *Tian Ting Zhi Bao*, *Yu Hu Gui Dan*, *Hunyuan Tai Xi*, and other spurious doctrines, recklessly distorting the text and failing to grasp its deep roots and firm foundations. There was also Ye Wenshu, who attempted to explain it through the numbers of the *Tai Ji* and *Da Yan*, even appending diagrams to the volume’s end, calling it *Wu Zhen Pian Wai Bian*. Thus, he mistook shadows for substance and horses for trunks, not only disordering the true scripture but also leading scholars further into confusion.

The immortal elder once said:
*"None are willing to reflect on their own errors,
Instead, they teach others the wrong path,
Leading them astray for eternity in the sea of delusion.
How could one bear such deceit?"*
Is this not a description of Wenshu?

Little did he know that the single grain of golden elixir is none other than the *qi* of the *Tai Ji*. The sage employs the two substances of true yin and true yang to capture this *qi* in a single moment, transforming it into a pearl-like grain. It is produced and ingested within a single *shichen*, instantly transcending to the realm of sages. Thus, the immortal elder said:
*"Within a single *shichen*, the elixir is complete,"*
and
*"Swallowing a single grain of the numinous elixir,
Only then do I know my fate is not ruled by heaven."*
How could these be empty words?

Wenshu, failing to comprehend this principle, dismissed the notion of "a single moment." If one insists that the process cannot be completed in a single moment, then it would require three years or even nine. How could this be the utmost simplicity and ease? Why would the immortal elder speak of *"a brief moment’s effort yielding eternal, boundless joy"*?

He further misconstrued "a single grain" as "a grain per day," citing the words of *Zhen Yi Zi*—*"consume one grain per day"*—as evidence, utterly misunderstanding *Zhen Yi Zi*’s intent. If one were to follow his logic—consuming one grain per day, with each day producing a new grain—then over three or nine years, one would ingest over a thousand grains. How could the immortal elder have deceived others by speaking of only a single grain?

These two points alone suffice to show that Wenshu did not grasp the art of the golden elixir. Thus, his annotations are riddled with errors, and he failed to recognize that the numbers of the *Tai Ji* and *Da Yan* are merely symbolic allusions to the fire phases—superficially plausible but fundamentally flawed. To seek the ultimate Dao of the golden elixir through such symbols is like trying to summon rain by painting dragons or satisfy hunger by drawing cakes. Is this not futile?

Though I am not particularly wise, I have followed in the immortal elder’s footsteps and received the true transmission. How could I remain silent, watching as the red and purple obscure the vermilion, without spreading the elder’s intent, refuting errors, and rescuing future generations from confusion? Thus, undeterred by heavenly retribution, I boldly reveal the celestial mechanism and elucidate the true text, strictly adhering to the immortal elder’s secret transmission:

To refine the great medicine of the golden elixir, first clarify the primordial *qi* of the undivided chaos before heaven and earth separated—this is the foundation of the elixir. Next, distinguish true yin and true yang, the mutually resonant and sentient substances, each weighing eight taels, to establish the furnace and tripod. Using the true *qi* of this furnace and tripod, employ symbolic methods and celestial movements to lure the primordial *qi* of the prior heaven. In no more than half a *shichen*, a single grain of elixir forms within the tripod, as small as a millet seed—this is the golden elixir.

Ingest this single grain of golden elixir, and it will subdue the essence and *qi* of the entire body, like a cat seizing a mouse or a goshawk snatching a bird, leaving them no escape. Then, circulate the true *qi* of yin and yang—termed yin talismans and yang fire—to nurture the essence and *qi*, transforming them into the substance of golden liquid. Suddenly, a substance surges from the *weilü*, ascending through the *jiaji* double pass with a distinct sound, rising to the *niwan*, striking the upper palate, and descending into the mouth in droplets like sparrow eggs, fragrant and sweet—this is the golden liquid returning elixir.

Slowly swallow it into the cinnabar field, where it coalesces into the sacred embryo. After ten months, the embryo is complete, the fire sufficient, and the body sheds its form, bathing and transforming into a body of pure yang, free from hunger, thirst, cold, heat, or harm from blades, tigers, or rhinoceroses. Thus, one becomes a terrestrial immortal, retreating to a secluded place to sit in stillness for nine years, emptying the mind—this is called embracing the One. After nine years of practice, both form and spirit naturally attain perfection, nature and life unite, and one merges with the true Dao, becoming unfathomable in transformation.

This is the ninefold golden liquid great returning elixir. Though the elixir is divided into three stages, they are fundamentally one, progressing from lesser to middle, and from middle to greater—this is the sequence of refining the elixir and entering the Dao.

Therefore, I have divided the text into three volumes:
- The **upper volume** refines the golden elixir as the art of strengthening the army and securing victory,
- The **middle volume** circulates the fire talismans and golden liquid returning elixir as the method of enriching the nation and pacifying the people,
- The **lower volume** presents the ninefold great returning elixir as the Dao of the immortal embracing the One.

This is called the **Three Vehicles Great Method**, corresponding to the true meaning of the *Yin Fu Jing*. It is the supreme, limitless, highest-grade celestial immortal’s foremost discipline—the most wondrous and true Dao. The five masters and ancestral teachers transmitted this method orally, and it is the sole practice.

The essence is most simple and most easy, requiring no more than a few words. Yet the sages, fearing the revelation of celestial secrets, employed myriad metaphors—such as the Qian-Kun crucible and furnace, dragon and tiger, lead and mercury—along with countless other esoteric names, all serving but to illustrate the imagery of the golden elixir. Here, I have compiled these variant names, pointing to each with utmost precision, leaving nothing, however minute, unaddressed. Yet, fearing that learners might not grasp the subtleties of their application, I have expounded in exhaustive detail, composing the *Wuzhen Zhizhi* to elucidate the three vehicles’ secret essentials. The *Three Vehicles’ Secret Essentials Treatise* and the *Three Vehicles’ Secret Essentials Poems* are appended at the end of the volume, exhausting the profound and subtle—one might say, fully exposing the true mechanism of heaven and earth, wholly unveiling the immortals’ guarded secrets.

May this be bequeathed to like-minded seekers, that they may delve deeply, perceive clearly, and directly attain the truth of *Wuzhen*, forever holding it as the orthodox Dharma-seal, unconfused by the heterodox teachings of deviant schools.

Written in the mid-autumn of the *gui-si* year of the Qiandao reign of the Great Song, by Weng Baoguang of Xiangchuan, the Nameless One, with reverence.

Master Kongxuan says: The Nameless One divided the text into three volumes, calling it the *Three Vehicles Great Method*, to correspond with the true meaning of the *Yin Fu Jing*. This is fundamentally based on the *Jinhua Master’s Explanation of the Yin Fu Jing*. The *Jinhua Explanation* states: "The art of strengthening the army and securing victory is the art of refining the golden liquid to transmute the form. It is the accumulation of yang hun to dissolve yin po, using yang soldiers to battle and repel yin bandits. Through the mutual overcoming of the five phases and the mutual agitation of the eight trigrams, one returns to the root and restores life, resplendently forming the elixir. The returning elixir is refined a hundredfold, its essence lying in the divine water of the Flowery Pond. Humans, born between heaven and earth, ignorant of heaven and earth’s principles, are robbed by yin evil. Time swiftly passes, spirit and essence dissipate, leading to decay. Thus, the sages devised this imagery to lure the true yang qi, congealing it into the divine elixir, prolonging and preserving the form. When spirit and Dao unite, it is like a nation beset by bandits: one must first strengthen the army to suppress them, cherishing its treasures to enrich the nation. Only after abundance is achieved can one practice the Dao of the immortal embracing the One."

**Eulogy:**
The Flowery Pond’s divine water, jade mercury and golden lead,
Qian and Kun provide the means, sun and moon the firing and refining.
Layering and guarding, igniting the fire, advancing and retreating,
Refining with precision, drawing and adding, bathing in the ninefold cycle.
A year of Kan and Li, the head of the work,
Three thousand meritorious deeds—hold fast, never slacken, and the form shall transcend.

The method of enriching the nation and pacifying the people is the art of refining qi. The *Laozi Neijing* states: "The structure of the body mirrors that of a nation." The sage treats the body as a nation, the heart as its ruler, and essence and qi as its people. When the people are at peace, the nation is prosperous; when the people scatter, the nation is desolate. Thus, cherishing essence and treasuring qi leads to longevity. Just as a ruler loves the people as his own children, constantly devising ways to nurture them, ensuring the people’s peace and the nation’s abundance—this is the method of enriching the nation and pacifying the people.

The human form is constituted from the father’s essence and mother’s blood, transformed by primordial qi. The *Zhonghuang Master* says: "Bones and flesh take essence and blood as their root; numinous awareness takes primordial qi as its foundation. Spirit and qi are the root of life. Spirit is the child of qi; qi is the mother of spirit—this is the mother-child within the form. When mother and child are never parted, qi abides serenely within the cinnabar terrace, naturally effecting transformations."

**Eulogy:**
The Gate of Heaven ever open, the Earth’s Door must be shut,
Ever preserve the unbroken thread, never allowing the slightest lapse.
Inhaling to the root, exhaling to the stem,
The child is called spirit, the mother qi.
Like a hen brooding her eggs, like a fish in water,
The sacred embryo forms, naturally shedding the shell.

The Dao of the immortal embracing the One is the art of refining spirit. It is the Dao of original spontaneity. Those who encounter the Dao, ever able to embrace the One and guard the center, attain spirit and qi undispersed, roaming freely as true persons.

**Eulogy:**
The Dao is the root of nature, nature the source of the heart,
Heart and nature are one body, responding and transforming boundlessly.
The common folk’s daily use is also called spontaneity.
If one can awaken, forget images, forget words,
Empty the heart and fill the belly, embrace the One and ascend—
After ninefold refinement, one attains immortality.

It is also said: Practicing the art of refining spirit leads to becoming a spirit immortal—where the form sinks and the spirit transcends, attaining free roaming. Practicing the art of refining qi leads to becoming an embryonic immortal—where the refined form’s spirit and qi intermingle, harmoniously uniting to gestate the embryo and nurture the infant, like the cicada shedding its shell. Practicing the art of refining the form is the method of reversing old age and restoring youth, preserving the form and abiding in the world.

With reverence, I have set forth these words to clarify the Nameless One’s original intent in dividing the text into three volumes. In other editions, the Nameless One is styled Yuanming.

**Preface to the *Wuzhen Pian*:**
Alas! The human body is hard to obtain, time swiftly passes, and the brevity of life is unfathomable. How can one escape karmic retribution without early awakening? Yet, many willingly wait for the end. If, at the final moment, a single thought errs, one instantly falls into the three evil destinies, where eons may pass without deliverance. At such a time, though one may repent, what use is it? Thus, the elders of the Lao and Shi traditions opened the gate of expedient means through the study of nature and life, teaching people to cultivate the seed of liberation and escape birth and death. The Shi tradition takes emptiness and quiescence as its essence; if one awakens suddenly and attains perfect penetration, one directly transcends the other shore. Yet, if habitual tendencies remain unexhausted, one still wanders in the realm of existence. The Lao tradition takes refinement and nurturance as the truth; if one grasps its pivotal essence, one immediately ascends to sagehood. If one has not awakened to original nature, one remains entangled in illusory form. Next, the *Zhouyi* contains sayings on exhausting principle, fulfilling nature, and reaching life; the *Lunyu* contains teachings on "no deliberation, no certainty, no stubbornness, no self." These are Confucius’ ultimate attainments in the mysteries of nature and life. Yet, why are his words often brief and lacking in detail? It is because he wished to order human relationships and implement the teachings of benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and music—expedient teachings. Thus, he did not openly expound the Dao of non-action, but embedded life techniques within the imagery of the *Yijing* and interwove nature’s methods within subtle words.

Later, Zhuangzi thoroughly explored the burdens of things and the nature of carefree wandering; Mencius excelled in nurturing the vast qi—both came close to the crux. Then, during the Han, Wei Boyang drew upon the *Yijing*’s principles of yin-yang union to compose the *Cantong Qi*, elucidating the workings of the great elixir. In the Tang, the Loyal National Preceptor, in the preface to his recorded sayings, expounded the words of Laozi and Zhuangzi to reveal the origin and end of the supreme Dao. Was this not because, though the teachings diverge into three paths, they ultimately converge as one? Yet, how lamentable that later generations of the yellow-robed and black-robed, each clinging to their own school, mutually denigrated one another, causing the essentials of the three traditions to become obscured. Heterodox teachings could not be harmonized and unified.

Moreover, today’s practitioners of the Dao value the cultivation of life yet do not understand that the methods of cultivating life arise from two approaches: those easy to encounter but difficult to accomplish, and those difficult to encounter but easy to accomplish. Methods such as refining the five sprouts’ qi, ingesting the seven luminaries’ light, visualization, massage, inhaling the pure and exhaling the turbid, reciting scriptures, holding mantras, spraying water, shouting talismans, clacking teeth, gathering spirits, abandoning wives, fasting, preserving spirit, holding the breath, directing thoughts between the brows, replenishing the brain, returning essence, and practicing bedroom arts—along with ingesting and refining minerals, herbs, and woods—are all easy to encounter but difficult to accomplish. The above methods, in relation to the Dao of cultivating the body, are generally fragmented. Though one may exert much effort, seeking results proves futile. Even with diligent practice day and night, one may only ward off illness and avoid misfortune. Yet, if one ceases practice even briefly, previous efforts gradually dissipate. This is to delay over years, making success nearly impossible. To hope for a single attainment, eternal attainment, returning to infancy, reversing old age, transformation, and ascension—is this not difficult indeed?

Deeply lamentable indeed—most modern practitioners cling stubbornly to delusions, failing to awaken to the truth of the wondrous method, and instead reproach the immortals for deceit. Little do they know that all who attain the Dao do so through the refinement of the Golden Elixir, yet fearing the leakage of celestial secrets, they veil it beneath various practices. Among these, only the method of breath-halting bears resemblance: if one can forget all scheming and quiet the mind, it aligns closely with the seated meditation of the Two Vehicles. Diligently practiced, it may lead to samadhi and spirit departure. Yet, as spirit belongs to yin and the dwelling is hard to secure, one must constantly employ shifting methods. Without mastering the Way of returning mercury and restoring lead, how can one reverse yang, renew the bones, and ascend to heaven in broad daylight?

The refinement of the Golden Liquor and Returned Elixir, though difficult to encounter, is easy to accomplish—provided one thoroughly comprehends yin and yang and deeply penetrates the workings of creation. Only then can one pursue the two qi along the Yellow Path, guard the three natures within the Primordial Palace, gather the five phases, harmonize the four images, where dragon chants and tiger roars, husband leads and wife follows, the jade crucible simmers, and the golden furnace blazes. Thus the Mysterious Pearl takes form, the Great One returns to truth—all achieved in but a moment’s effort, ensuring boundless joy and eternal bliss. Yet one must vigilantly guard against peril, weigh risks, exercise caution in operation, withdrawal, and replenishment, nurture the upright, and maintain fullness. By embracing femininity and holding to the One, one naturally reverts to the state of infancy, sheds the yin-killing form, completes the cyclical nodes of qi, casts off the mortal husk, and undergoes spiritual transformation. Then one’s name is inscribed in the immortal registers, and the title of Perfected One is conferred—this is the moment when a great man’s work is accomplished and his name established.

Yet today’s scholars mistake lead and mercury for the two qi, designate the viscera as the five phases, divide heart and kidney into Kan and Li, take liver and lung as dragon and tiger, employ spirit and qi as mother and child, and cling to fluids as lead and mercury. Unaware of floating and sinking, unable to distinguish host and guest, they are no different from mistaking another’s wealth for their own or calling a stranger’s child their kin. How can they fathom the subtle interplay of metal and wood’s mutual restraint or the profound mystery of yin and yang’s reciprocal use? With sun and moon straying from the Dao, lead and mercury placed in separate crucibles, how can they hope to congeal the Returned Elixir? Is this not difficult indeed?

From youth, I delighted in the Dao, traversing the Three Teachings’ scriptures, as well as those on penal codes, mathematics, medicine, divination, warfare, astronomy, geography, and the arts of fortune and misfortune, death and life—all of which I studied with care and scrutinized in detail. Yet of the Golden Elixir alone, though I perused all the classics and the songs, poems, discourses, and treatises of various schools—all speaking of the sun’s essence and moon’s spirit, the metal tiger and wood dragon, mercury, cinnabar, white gold, and black tin, Kan’s male and Li’s female, capable of forming the Golden Liquor and Returned Elixir—none revealed what true lead and true mercury are, nor explained the methods of firing, the degrees of refinement, the nurturing of the elixir, or its ultimate return. Later, deluded disciples arbitrarily imposed their own interpretations upon the sacred teachings of the ancients, distorting them in myriad ways, not only disordering the immortal classics but also misleading later learners.

As an ordinary man, I had yet to encounter the oral transmission, and such secrets were hard to come by. Thus, I grew restless, my spirit weary, my energy exhausted. Though I sought counsel far and wide across mountains and seas, and inquired of both the wise and foolish, none could clarify the true essence. Only after the true teaching illuminated my heart did I, in the *jisi* and *you* years of the Xining era, follow Minister Lu into Chengdu. Unwavering in my original resolve, I redoubled my devotion and was moved to receive from a Perfected One the oral instructions on the Golden Elixir’s ingredients, firing times, and methods. His words were concise, his essentials uncluttered—truly, to point to the stream is to know the source; a single utterance awakens a hundred insights, dispersing the mist like the sun’s brilliance, clearing the dust like a mirror’s clarity. Comparing this with the immortal classics, they matched as if sealed together.

I observed that of those who study immortality, eight or nine out of ten do so, yet scarcely one or two grasp its true essentials. Having encountered the true transmission, how could I dare remain silent? Thus, I poured forth all I had received, composing ninety-nine regulated verses, numbering them *Enlightenment on Reality*. Among these, sixteen heptasyllabic quatrains correspond to the number of two eights; sixty-four quatrains follow the hexagrams of the *Zhouyi*; one pentasyllabic verse symbolizes the singularity of the Great One. I later appended twelve *Moon over the West River* lyrics to align with the twelve months. Within these, the hierarchy of the crucible, the weights of the ingredients, the progression and retreat of the firing, the sequence of host and guest, and the conditions of existence and nonexistence, fortune and misfortune, regret and remorse—all are fully encompassed.

After compiling this work, I realized it spoke only of the arts of nurturing life and solidifying form, leaving the original source and true awakening of the nature unexplored. Thus, I delved into Buddhist scriptures and the *Records of the Transmission of the Lamp*, where I found that ancestral masters had awakened upon hearing bamboo struck, and expressed their realizations in songs, odes, poems, and miscellaneous verses. I composed thirty-two such pieces and appended them to the end of the volume, hoping that the Way of realizing the root and illuminating the nature might be fully expounded herein.

May those of like aspiration who peruse this work perceive the end and awaken to the root, abandon delusion and follow truth.

Written on the first day of the *yimao* year of the Xining era of the august Song dynasty by Zhang Boduan, styled Pingshu, of Tiantai.

*Imperially Commissioned Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*
*Annotated and Elucidated Wuzhen Pian, Volume One*
Composed by Zhang Boduan of the Song
Annotated by Weng Baoguang
Elucidated by Dai Qizong of the Yuan

**Seven-Character Quatrains (Sixteen Poems to Represent the Number One Jin and Two Eights)**

Not seeking the Great Dao, one strays from the path—
Though burdened with talent, how can one be a true man?
A hundred years of light pass swift as spark from flint,
A lifetime’s fate drifts like bubbles on the stream.

Only craving rank and wealth, chasing fleeting glory,
Unseen, the body withers, parched and gaunt.
If gold were piled as high as mountains,
Could it buy escape from impermanence when death arrives?

**Annotation:** In the human world, what is most prized are wealth and nobility—both coveted by all. Thus, people exhaust their nature and life’s purpose, striving with all their might to attain them, deeming success their greatest joy. Yet what drives this pursuit is nothing more than immersion in profit, rank, sensuality, and desire. Little do they know that profit, rank, and sensual pleasures are but spears that sever life and shackles that bind the body.

Ordinary folk, ignorant of the wonders of Dao, virtue, nature, and life, gluttonously chase profit, drowning in delusion, swept away by the river of love, adrift in the sea of desire. Right and wrong, self and other wage war in their hearts; joy, anger, sorrow, and pleasure ravage their frames. Thus, demonic forces hasten their breath’s end, the seasons erode their vitality, and unknowing, they spend their lives like fleeting foam upon the water, their hundred years vanishing in an instant like sparks from stone. Even if gold were heaped like bushels or jade stacked like mountains, could it, at the hour of impermanence, buy back a body to spare it from becoming a nest for ants? Alas! What sorrow! What grief!

The life-force has yet to fully depart when the true spirit has already entered another husk.

Master Vacuous Tranquility said: "In this life, unawakened, one passes beyond and exchanges husks, entering another shell, revolving endlessly without awareness. Alas! Rather than unknowingly cast into a foreign form, how much better to dwell in the Great Dao? There, merit is perfected, the body ascends beyond the azure vault, riding cloud-qi and mounting flying dragons to wander the boundless. Death and life no longer alter—thus one attains the title of Perfected Person. This is the season of fulfillment for the true great man, the day of utmost honor and brilliance.

Yet those who, entangled in trivial worldly affairs and burdened by dust-laden hearts, fall into the snares of the mundane—though they possess the heroic talent of Ban and Sima, or the illustrious renown of Su and Zhang—are but wandering souls upon the earth, shadowy wraiths of walking corpses. How could they be deemed true great men?

Thus, the Immortal Elder first composed this chapter, speaking especially to those of lofty insight and exceptional clarity, who may awaken through a single word, seek the Great Dao, escape the paths of delusion, and engage in the work of non-action—these alone are the true great men. All else is unworthy of pursuit."

Disciple Qi Zong of the Contemplative School commented: "To read the Immortal Elder’s poem and Master Wuming’s annotations without awakening is truly the height of folly. Those with eyes to see and ears to hear—how can they fail to set their hands to cultivation?"

Though human life may span a hundred years, its length or brevity, its prosperity or adversity—none can be foreknown. Yesterday, one still galloped through the streets; today, one lies as a corpse within the coffin. Wife and wealth are left behind, not truly one’s own; only the karmic deeds one has committed will accompany one, impossible to deceive. If the Great Elixir is not sought, how can one hope to encounter it? And even if encountered, to fail in its refinement is the height of folly.

The annotation states: The length or brevity of a person’s life, their prosperity or adversity, wealth or poverty, nobility or lowliness—none are not silently determined before they come to pass. How could ordinary people fathom or foresee them? Thus, though human life is said to span a hundred years, by the time one reaches seventy, it is already rare. With this fragile, easily destroyed body, to spend each day in ceaseless toil, chasing after boundless and unpredictable affairs—is this not difficult indeed? When a single breath fails to return, one departs forever, not knowing where one goes—can this not be called a great sorrow?

At such a time, though one may have attained the highest rank, enjoyed boundless emoluments, possessed priceless treasures, or been surrounded by beauties of unmatched allure—all must be left behind, for none are truly one’s own. The only thing that accompanies one, impossible to deceive, is the karmic deeds one has accumulated in life. Thus it is said: "A thousand things will not go with you; only karma follows the body."

Master Cao’s verse says:
*Alas, how hurried and chaotic is human life,*
*Entangled daily in the fire-house of worldly dust.*
*Suddenly, when the three-inch breath in the throat is cut,*
*Body and soul, spirit and hun—all scatter and disperse.*
*No matter how your wife and children wail and call,*
*A thousand cries, ten thousand pleas—you will not turn your head.*
*Though you may have schemed and calculated all your life,*
*Lying in the barren mound, you lose your wits at last.*
Truly, these words are so.

For those who wish to escape the cycle of rebirth and avoid entanglement in the world’s snares, nothing surpasses the Golden Elixir as the numinous ladder to ascend to heaven and the swift path to transcend the mundane. Its Way is most simple and most easy; even the dullest and most foolish may practice it and swiftly ascend to sagehood. Yet the highest sages guard it closely, forbidding its careless disclosure. Those who transmit it do so only through direct oral instruction, never committing it to writing—thus it is rarely encountered. Unless one seeks it with diligence, a steadfast will, and sincere devotion to move the heavens, one may never hear even a hint of it.

In former times, Xie Ziran, finding this Way so difficult to encounter, sought a true master in the Penglai Isles. He exhausted his family’s wealth to prepare a boat, heedless of the peril of towering waves and vast tides, pressing forward without the slightest fear. Thereupon, a divine being was moved to speak to him, saying: "The Penglai Isles are separated by thirty thousand *li* of weak water, upon which not even a mustard seed may float. Where do you intend to go? On the Red City Mountain, there is Sima Ziwei—go and take him as your teacher." Xie turned his boat and sought out Red City Mountain, where he indeed encountered Ziwei, who transmitted the Way to him. After refining it for no more than a few years, he ascended to heaven in broad daylight.

Ah! When a person’s sincerity bursts forth from within, moving and resonating without, what encounter could be impossible? If one can hold fast to the heart and uphold the will in this manner, how could one fail to obtain the Golden Elixir? The Way does not betray people; people betray the Way.

The *Cantong Qi* says:
*The Great Dao is not partial;*
*It is only transmitted to the worthy.*
If one does not exert utmost sincerity and ardent aspiration, how could one encounter it?

The secret essentials of the Golden Elixir are indeed difficult to encounter. Those who do encounter it all possess innate immortal bones and the accumulated profound merits of their ancestors. Yet even so, one must also have the financial means to gather three fellow cultivators before this work can be accomplished—this principle is understood only by those of insight. Truly, it is difficult to explain in full.

Master Yin’s *Six-Five Subtleties* says: "To learn this Way, one must have resources. Without resources, the Golden Elixir cannot be completed." Furthermore, three people are required for its refinement. Thus, when Chongxi Wangjun encountered Liu Haichan and obtained the art of the Golden Elixir, lacking the means to begin, he entered Luoyang to seek the wealthy Han Gong. Relying on his assistance, he completed the Way and departed.

If one encounters this Way yet fails to cultivate it with like-minded companions of means, one is truly the height of folly. The Immortal Elder’s poem to Master Liu says:
*I hear you have known the medicine for many years—*
*Why not gather your heart and refine mercury and lead?*
*Do not let the candle be blown out by the wind.*
*The six paths of rebirth—blame not heaven.*
This is the same meaning.

The commentary states: In the eleventh year of the Zhenyuan era, on the twelfth day of the first month, the Perfected of Guozhou, Xie Ziran, ascended in broad daylight at Golden Spring Mountain outside the western gate of the prefectural city. The entire populace witnessed it. The prefect, Li Jian, reported the matter to the throne, and a stele was erected to record it. The emperor issued an edict in praise, saying: "In this region, numinous immortals manifest their wonders, and the mystic wind flourishes ever more. The supreme Way spreads ever wider."

Han Changli’s poem says:
*In Guozhou’s Nanchong County, the poor scholar Xie Ziran,*
*A child who knew nothing, heard only of immortals.*
*Light of heart, he learned their arts on Golden Spring Mountain.*
*One morning, he sat in empty space; clouds and mist rose from the cliffs,*
*As if the music of pipes and strings came from the void.*
*In an instant, he ascended lightly, drifting like smoke in the clouds.*
*The prefect, astonished, led officials and common folk in haste,*
*But when they entered the gate, nothing remained—*
*Only his cap and shoes, like a cicada’s shed husk.*
*Of old, the affairs of immortals were true—*
*This is certain proof to transmit.*

Wang Bohou’s *Kunxue Jiwen* records: Wang Quan, styled Zizhen, was a man of Yangping in Qixia. In the Yuanfeng era, he was granted the title "Chongxi Recluse." Zhang Yunyou composed a stele inscription for his meritorious deeds, saying: "His transcendent bearing, his companionship with Chen Tunan and Master Cheng Yi—yet one never heard him compose poetry, save for a verse sent to Wang Zizhen:
*I too have the Elixir—do you believe it?*
*When the time comes, can it still aid the people?*
When Master Cheng Yi entered Mount Song, Zizhen awaited him beneath the pines, asking: "How did you know I would come?" He replied: "Last year, I already received word." For the Master had intended to go the previous year but was detained by affairs.

The *You Zhai Man Lu* also records: Wang Quan, styled Zizhen, was a man of the Dao. Fu Zhenggong once lodged with him. In the Yuanfeng era, the emperor bestowed upon him the title "Chongxi Recluse." In the third year of the Yuanfu era, he journeyed to Mount Mao to receive the Shangqing Register. Previously, the stone cavern on the central peak of Mount Mao had suddenly opened—it was the side gate of the Huayang Grotto-Heaven. Since the immortal Zuo Yuanfang had departed, it had been sealed for a thousand years. Now it opened again. For days beforehand, sweet dew descended. The Daoist Liu Hun Kang said: "This must portend something extraordinary." Soon after, the Master arrived. On the night he received the Register, celestial music was heard in the empty sky above. The mountain inscribed a stele to record the event.

---

To learn immortality, one must learn celestial immortality;
Only the Golden Elixir is the truest path.
When the two things meet in due season, the nature and emotions unite;
The Five Agents are complete, where tiger and dragon coil.
The root cause lies in *wu-si* as the matchmaker;
Thus husband and wife attain joyous union.
Wait only for the work’s completion to ascend the Jade Palace,
Amidst the nine rosy clouds, riding a soaring simurgh.

A commentary states: Among immortals there are several ranks. Those spirits most numinous yet formless are *gui*-immortals. Those who dwell in the world free from all illness and vexation, enjoying long life, are human immortals. Those who soar through the sky and walk through mist, untroubled by hunger or thirst, unharmed by cold or heat, who roam the island paradises and live forever without dying, are earthly immortals. Those whose form and spirit are both wondrous, who merge with the Dao and become true, who walk beneath the sun and moon without casting a shadow, who pass through metal and stone without hindrance, whom water and fire cannot burn or drown, whose transformations are boundless—now old, now young, now hidden, now manifest, now present, now absent—who disperse to become *qi* and coalesce to take form, beyond the divination of milfoil and tortoiseshell, beyond the knowledge of gods and spirits, are celestial immortals.

Master Yin Zhen says: If one can extinguish desire, cultivate embryonic breathing, shift the spirit, shed the husk, enter samadhi, project the corpse, and rely on the Yin to transform without decay, one may become a lower-rank immortal. Those who receive the talismans of the Six Jias, the registers of Zhengyi Mengwei, Shangqing, and the Three Caverns, along with methods such as sword techniques and corpse liberation, and thereby attain the Dao, are all listed among the immortals of the Southern Palace, dwelling in the various grotto-heavens as middle-rank immortals. Those who refine the Golden Elixir and the Great Medicine, and thereby attain the Dao, ascend bodily into the heavens as the supreme, limitless celestial immortals of the highest rank.

Thus the immortal elders exhort practitioners of the true to establish resolve, be bold and untrammeled, stand apart from the crowd, and transcend the middle and lower ranks of immortals to become the supreme, limitless celestial immortals of the highest rank. Among the seventy-two grades of elixirs, the path to celestial immortality is simple and easily accomplished—only the Golden Elixir is the truest and most direct Way.

This is the creation of something from nothing. It is not the five metals, eight stones, cinnabar, mercury, black lead, white tin, yellow dan, realgar, orpiment, arsenic, powdered frost, verdigris, chalcanthite, autumn stone, plant ashes, frost, dregs, or any of the myriad substances refined through boiling and sublimation—nor even one’s own saliva, essence, *qi*, blood, or fluids—all of which are the creation of something from something. Only the *qi* of the primordial is the *qi* of the undifferentiated chaos before Heaven and Earth separated, the true One of the primordial origin, called the creation of something from nothing. The sage, through method, gathers and captures it within a single *chen*, condensing it into a grain the size of a millet seed, called the Golden Elixir, also known as true lead, yang elixir, the essence of the true One, the water of the true One, true water, the water tiger, or the *qi* of the Great One containing true essence. One who obtains a single grain and ingests it ascends immediately to the realm of the sages—this is the highest imperial examination, the great Way of celestial immortality.

Few in the world know of it. The *qi* of the true One was born before Heaven and Earth, mingling within the formless void, indistinct and elusive—seen but not visible, heard but not audible, grasped but not attainable. How then can it be condensed into a millet seed? The sage gives form to the formless through the substantial and gives shape to the shapeless through the existent. What is substantial and existent is true Yin and true Yang—both are sentient beings of the same kind. What is formless and insubstantial is the *qi* of the initial union of the two eights, which has *qi* but no substance. When these two take form, one thing is born from them. That one thing is the *qi* of the true One, coagulated into a pearl the size of a millet seed.

The scripture says: "In the beginning, the Primordial suspended a precious pearl, large as a millet seed, within the empty void"—this is its proof. Fearing the leakage of Heaven and Earth’s mechanism, the sages used true Yin and true Yang as metaphors for the Azure Dragon and White Tiger, and the *qi* of the two unions as metaphors for true lead and true mercury.

Now, the immortal elders in their poetic verses further designate the single entity of the Azure Dragon with names such as the Crimson Dragon, the Thunder Dragon, the Celestial Soul, the Qian House, the Qian Crucible, the Jade Tripod, Fusang, the Jade Pond, the lower union’s half-moon, Eastern Yang, the Eldest Son, Vermilion Mercury, the Cinnabar Tripod, the Li Sun, and the Scarlet Phoenix—yet all these are but alternate names for the Azure Dragon. Likewise, they designate the single entity of the White Tiger with names such as the Black Tiger, the Dui Tiger, the Terrestrial Soul, the Kun Position, the Kun Tripod, the Metal Tripod, the Metal Crucible, Mount Hua, the prior union’s half-moon, the Western River, the Youngest Daughter, Black Lead, the Crescent-Moon Crucible, the Kan Moon, and the Black Tortoise—yet all these are but alternate names for the White Tiger.

Moreover, they refer to the *qi* of the Dragon as true mercury, the Alchemical Maiden, the Watery Essence, the Azure Maiden, mercury within cinnabar, mercury within vermilion, essence, the Yellow Sprout, the Flowing Pearl, the Azure-Clad Maiden, the Golden Crow, the Li Maiden, the Female Dragon, true fire, the sixteen-year-old Alchemical Maiden, the Jade Fluid, and the Jade Mushroom—though in truth, these are one and the same. Similarly, they name the *qi* of the Tiger as true lead, the Metal Duke, the Metal Essence, gold within water, silver within water, nature, the Golden Flower, White Snow, the White-Clad Gentleman, the Jade Hare, the Kan Son, true water, the Nine-Three Gentleman, the Male Tiger, and the Knife-Tip—though in truth, these are one and the same.

When these two entities interact, their nature and temperament unite: these two are the Dragon and Tiger. The Azure Dragon resides in the East, where the East pertains to Wood, and Wood generates Fire; thus, the *qi* of the Dragon is Fire, termed "temperament," and belongs to the South, where it is called the Vermilion Bird. The White Tiger resides in the West, where the West pertains to Metal, and Metal generates Water; thus, the *qi* of the Tiger is Water, termed "nature," and belongs to the North, where it is called the Dark Warrior. Now, the Dragon is Wood, the Tiger is Metal, nature is Water, and temperament is Fire—these are the Four Symbols. When the Four Symbols gather at the Central Palace to form the elixir, that which unites them is Earth. These constitute the true Five Phases.

When the Dragon and Tiger unite, their nature and temperament harmonize; when the Dragon and Tiger conjoin to form the elixir, the Five Phases are complete. Thus it is said: "When the two entities interact, nature and temperament unite, and the Five Phases are complete—there the Tiger and Dragon coil." The Wood Dragon resides in the East, the Metal Tiger in the West; between these two entities lies a separation—who can cause them to pair and become husband and wife? Their pairing depends solely on the Yellow Dame. With her left hand, she seizes the Dragon; with her right, she grasps the Tiger, causing them to unite. Since Wu and Ji pertain to Earth, she is called the Yellow Dame. Though Metal and Wood are separated, the Yellow Dame enables their union and pairing—how could this not be the matchmaker who brings them together in joyous matrimony?

These two are distinct, yet the *qi* of the true One is concealed within them; these two are one, for the *qi* of the true One transforms them. When the *qi* of the true One transforms, the true person naturally emerges—this is the symbolic method of the external medicine. After ingesting this Golden Elixir, one must further undertake the ten-month work of refining form into *qi*, and then the nine-year practice of embracing the One to transform *qi* into spirit. Only then can form and spirit both attain perfection, merging with the Dao in truth, receiving the celestial registers and ascending as a guest to Heaven. When the elixir ripens, the Way is accomplished in the human realm, and one ascends to Heaven, where amidst the nine rosy clouds, wind stirs beneath one’s arms, and one rides the phoenix and mounts the simurgh, soaring through the azure vault. Yet who among those who have not planted the spiritual root and amassed abundant hidden virtue could partake in this?

The commentary states: *The Transmission of the Dao and Collected Writings* says, "Immortals are not of one kind. Those purely Yin without Yang are *gui*; those purely Yang without Yin are immortals; those with both Yin and Yang intermingled are humans. Only humans can become *gui* or immortals. Among immortals, there are five ranks. The *gui*-immortal transcends Yin yet remains within it; their spirit-image is indistinct, their *gui*-pass lacks a surname, and across three lives, they have no name. Though they escape the cycle of rebirth, they struggle to return to Penglai; ultimately, they have no place to abide, resorting to possessing others’ bodies. This is because their spirit remains inwardly fixed, their mind undispersed, and their intent focused—thus they emit a Yin spirit, a numinous *gui* rather than a purely Yang immortal. Since their will is Yin and their spirit undispersed, they are called *gui*-immortals. The followers of Śramaṇa who cultivate to this point claim to have attained the Dao—how laughable!

The human immortal cannot fully embody the great Dao and is limited to the efficacy of a single method or art, enabling peaceful longevity. The earthly immortal grasps the principle of Heaven and Earth’s ascent and descent, employs the numerology of the Sun and Moon’s waxing and waning, and within the body uses years, months, and days, while within the day uses hours and moments. They recognize the Dragon and Tiger, pair Kan and Li, invert the Five Phases, circulate the *qi* of mother and child, and practice the union of husband and wife. Through repeated refinement, they form the elixir, which permanently resides in the lower field, refining their form to dwell in the world, achieving long life without death as an earthbound immortal.

The divine immortal, weary of dwelling in the dust, continues their practice without cease, refining form into image, causing the Five *Qi* to ascend to the origin, and when the three Yang gather at the crown, their work complete, they forget form, and the fetal immortal naturally transforms. With Yin exhausted and Yang pure, they gain a body beyond the body, shed their form, ascend as immortals, transcend the mundane, and enter sagehood, bidding farewell to the dust to return to the Three Mountains.

The celestial immortal, weary of dwelling on the Three Isles, transmits the Dao among humans. Above, they have merited the Dao; among humans, they have fulfilled their conduct. When their merit and conduct are complete, they receive Heaven’s decree to return to the Grotto-Heavens, ascending to the Eighty-One Yang Heavens, and further to the realm of the Three Pure Ones, the natural realm of numinous light.

There are those born of existence: they transform through tangible things to achieve this. Though they begin with form, how can they transform the tangible into the intangible? From non-existence, existence arises; from the formless, form is born. The form gives rise to the elixir, which, when ingested, can transform the tangible into the intangible, converting postnatal *qi* into prenatal *qi*. Form and spirit both attain perfection, for the formless is the *qi* of the true One, dwelling within the true Dragon and true Tiger, utterly without falsity. Those who grasp this mechanism and steal it can entice the *qi* of the true One to coagulate and generate form. Ingesting a single grain, they instantly ascend to sagehood—this is it. It is not through non-action, non-effort, sitting in meditation, or refining with ordinary fire and common medicines that one can succeed.

In essence, it is one thing, divided into two; within these two things, each contains Yin and Yang, forming the Four Symbols. The Four Symbols unite as one, with Earth at their center, constituting the true Five Phases. When the Five Phases unite as one, they return to their origin—this is the Golden Elixir. Before the entities unite, each conceals the *qi* of the true One within; when the two entities merge, they exchange the *qi* of the true One, and only then does the elixir form.

As the *Oral Transmission from Master Shangyan* states: "Regarding the two entities, each must steadfastly settle the mind and stabilize the work. When the time comes, the eyes will perceive, the heart will awaken, and the numinous light will appear as bright and pure as the moon—this is the white light born in an empty chamber."

Further refining the work requires witnessing the solar orb’s crimson radiance surge forth—only then does the white light vanish. When these two entities are fully realized, the foundation of the elixir is laid, and the Four are poised to coalesce. This method, true within the true, is yet more true, for I alone differ from others in knowing the inversion wrought by *Li* and *Kan*. Who discerns the floating and sinking, the settling of host and guest?

The golden crucible seeks to retain cinnabar within mercury; the jade pool must first receive silver from the waters. Through divine skill, fire is moved—not merely from dawn to dusk—until the abyssal pool reveals a solar orb.

**Annotation:** This Dao is most sagely, most numinous, most noble, most revered, most simple, most effortless—the profound within the profound, the marvelous within the marvelous. Rarely heard in the world, it emerges from the immortals, surpassing all in its purity. Thus did Master Chongxi say: "Of the great Dao of the Golden Elixir, none among the world’s Daoists merit approval—only Pingshu alone. He is Mount Tai, the rivers and seas; how dare the anthills and puddles compare?"

*Li* is Yang yet dwells in the south, thus paradoxically becoming the female, for it is Yang without but Yin within—this is true mercury. *Kan* is Yin yet dwells in the north, thus paradoxically becoming the male, for it is Yin without but Yang within—this is true lead. Hence the immortal’s saying: "The sun resides in *Li*’s position yet becomes the female; *Kan* pairs with the Toad Palace yet is the male." Do not obstruct this inverted intent with narrow views. This speaks of *Kan*’s male and *Li*’s female—like a father’s essence and a mother’s blood, the sun’s crow and the moon’s hare, cinnabar’s mercury and lead’s silver, Heaven’s mystery and Earth’s yellow. These all point to the initial stirring of Dragon and Tiger, the two *qi*.

Host and guest: Yang is noble, ascending to the left as host; Yin is lowly, descending to the right as guest. *Li* is fire, flaming upward, sharing Wood’s floating nature—thus Yang, thus host. *Kan* is water, moistening downward, sharing Metal’s sinking nature—thus Yin, thus guest. This is the ordinary way. Yet now, *Li* reverses to female, *Kan* to male—host becomes guest, guest becomes host. Is this not inversion? Thus it is said: "Only I know the inversion wrought by *Li* and *Kan*; who discerns the floating and sinking, the settling of host and guest?" Settling host and guest means taking the *qi* of the two stirrings within the Dao, inverting their host-guest roles, not adhering to the ordinary order—hence "settling host and guest."

The golden crucible: gold is a Yin substance, yet within the crucible lies the *qi* of fire—this is the image of Yin containing Yang, the White Tiger. The jade pool: jade is a Yang substance, yet within the pool lies the *qi* of water—this is the image of Yang containing Yin, the Green Dragon. Mercury within cinnabar is the Dragon’s stirring *qi*; silver within water is the Tiger’s stirring *qi*. The cultivator of the elixir, wishing to bind the Tiger’s *qi* to the Dragon’s, must first drive the Dragon to the Tiger. Only then do the two entities intermingle, their passions merging, their work of refinement proceeding—naturally congealing the essence of the true One.

Fire is the *qi* of the two stirrings. "Dawn" marks the beginning of the day; *Zi* is the origin of the six Yangs—hence "dawn." The sage moves the elixir fire with divine skill, and in less than half an hour, the essence of the true One is obtained—a grain like millet, appearing in the Northern Sea, its radiance piercing the curtains, like a red sun emerging from the depths of a deep pool. "Not merely from dawn to dusk" clarifies that within one *shichen*, the Golden Elixir is accomplished. This describes the external medicine’s symbolic form.

**Exegesis:** *Kan*’s male, *Li*’s female; the sun’s crow, the moon’s hare; Heaven’s mystery, Earth’s yellow. The golden crucible of Yin contains fire; the jade land of Yang holds water. Host reversed to guest, guest to host—all is taking Yang from Yin, Yin from Yang. Inverting the Five Phases, cultivating in reverse, one reaches the Supreme Ultimate. The Supreme Ultimate is the *qi* of the precelestial, undivided state. The Golden Elixir is congealed from Yang fire—not the ordinary fire of the world. The *qi* of the two stirrings is all fire. The two stirrings are their origin: *qi* without form, their source pure and unstained. In years, this is the balance of Yin and Yang at sixteen; in months, the equilibrium of Metal and Water at the crescent and gibbous phases.

Five—
The Tiger leaps, the Dragon soars, winds and waves surge rough.
At the central true position, the mystery pearl is born;
As fruit ripens on the branch, the child matures in the womb.
What difference lies between the Southern and Northern lineages?
The trigrams reverse, the fire times align with the celestial pivot.
Know that the great fish dwells in the marketplace—
Why seek seclusion in the deep mountains?

**Annotation:** This poem speaks of the inner medicine’s symbolic form. The essence of the true One, when manifest in the external, is called the Golden Elixir or true Earth. Swallowed into the belly, it becomes true lead, also named Yang Elixir—this refers to the Tiger as the Golden Elixir. The Dragon is one’s true *qi*, rising from the Sea of Qi like surging waves, moving like the wind. The central true position is the Golden Womb’s divine chamber within the Cinnabar Field, where the elixir gathers. The mystery pearl: when moving the fire, the true essence naturally circulates, ascending from the coccyx, piercing the spine, rushing to the Mud Pill Palace, then descending in droplets to the mouth—each like a sparrow’s egg, sweet and fragrant beyond compare. Called the mystery pearl, when swallowed into the Cinnabar Field, it is named the infant or the Golden Liquid Returning Elixir.

The millet-grain elixir is the *qi* of the precelestial, the essence of the true One congealed—it becomes the mother, the sovereign, the lead. Thus the *Golden Key* calls it black lead or water tiger. The true *qi* of *Si* is postcelestial, becoming the child, the minister, the mercury. Thus the *Golden Key* calls it red lead or fire dragon. The Golden Elixir comes from without; swallowed into the belly, one’s true *qi* surges upward from the lower Sea of Qi like wind and waves, converging upon the elixir—like a minister to a sovereign, a child to a mother, their mutual intent is evident. Dragon and Tiger unite in the divine chamber, the earthen crucible, receiving the fire’s nurturing to congeal the sacred embryo. As fruit must ripen and a child must be born, after ten months of work, the embryo is delivered, the spirit transformed without limit.

South and North refer to the *Zi* and *Wu* hours. Lineage origin refers to the very beginning.

At dawn and dusk begin the day and night. *Zi* is the first of the six yang, thus dawn, governed by the *Zhun* hexagram, the time for advancing fire. *Wu* is the origin of the six yin, thus dusk, governed by the *Meng* hexagram, the time for advancing water. Each day two hexagrams govern the work; by thirty days’ end, the *Jiji* and *Weiji* hexagrams conclude, then begin anew in endless cycle—hence the turning of hexagrams. The *Cantong Qi* says: “At the new moon’s dawn, *Zhun* governs; at dusk, *Meng* takes its turn.” Day and night each receive one hexagram, used in sequence. When the month’s end nears, the cycle recommences—this is the way.

Each day two hexagrams preside, with the four hexagrams of *Pin* and *Mou*; in a month, sixty-four hexagrams total, comprising 384 lines, corresponding to the year’s days, including intercalary months. *Qian*’s first nine begins with *Kun*’s first six; *Qian*’s tally is thirty-six lines, totaling 216. *Kun*’s first six begins with *Qian*’s first nine; *Kun*’s tally is twenty-four lines, totaling 144. Together they sum to 360, matching the celestial cycle. The movements of sun and moon—conjunction, ascent, descent—all unfold within the hexagrams’ lines. The moon traverses swiftly, completing one celestial cycle in a month; the sun moves slowly, completing one in a year. The Celestial Pivot is the pole of the Dipper’s handle.

In one day and night, the Dipper completes one revolution; each month, it shifts one position. Thus in the first month, it aligns with *Yin*; in the second, with *Mao*—this is the way. For example, in the first month, aligned with *Yin*: if the sun has not yet passed the palace division, *Yin* is placed at *Hai*, extending to *You*, establishing *Zi*—the first month’s Dipper aligns with *Zi* at the *You* hour. If the sun has passed the palace division, *Yin* is placed at *Xu*, extending to *Yin*, establishing *Wu*—the first month’s Dipper aligns with *Wu* at the *Yin* hour.

The superior adept, the perfected one, comprehends yin and yang above and below, knows the waxing and waning of sun and moon, and the fire tally of *Zi* and *Wu*.
